               </div>
               <div class="section abstract" id="abstract-1" itemprop="description">
                  <div class="section-nav">
                     <div class="nav-placeholder"> </div><a href="#sec-5" title="Methods" class="next-section-link"><span>Next Section</span></a></div>
                  <h2>Abstract</h2>
                  
                  <div id="sec-1" class="subsection">
                     
                     
                     <p id="p-1"><strong>Background</strong> Colorectal cancer is a major global public health problem, with approximately 950 000 patients newly diagnosed each year.
                        We report the first comprehensive field synopsis and creation of a parallel publicly available and regularly updated database
                        (CRCgene) that catalogs all genetic association studies on colorectal cancer (<a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/</a>).
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
                  <div id="sec-2" class="subsection materials"> 
                     
                     <p id="p-2"><strong>Methods</strong> We performed two independent systematic reviews, reviewing 10 145 titles, then collated and extracted data from 635 publications
                        reporting on 445 polymorphisms in 110 different genes. We carried out meta-analyses to derive summary effect estimates for
                        92 polymorphisms in 64 different genes. For assessing the credibility of associations, we applied the Venice criteria and
                        the Bayesian False Discovery Probability (BFDP) test.
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
                  <div id="sec-3" class="subsection">
                     
                     
                     <p id="p-3"><strong>Results</strong> We consider 16 independent variants at 13 loci (<em>MUTYH, MTHFR, SMAD7,</em> and common variants tagging the loci 8q24, 8q23.3, 11q23.1, 14q22.2, 1q41, 20p12.3, 20q13.33, 3q26.2, 16q22.1, and 19q13.1)
                        to have the most highly credible associations with colorectal cancer, with all variants except those in <em>MUTYH</em> and 19q13.1 reaching genome-wide statistical significance in at least one meta-analysis model. We identified less-credible
                        (higher heterogeneity, lower statistical power, BFDP &gt;0.2) associations with 23 more variants at 22 loci. The meta-analyses
                        of a further 20 variants for which associations have previously been reported found no evidence to support these as true associations.
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
                  <div id="sec-4" class="subsection">
                     
                     <p id="p-4"><strong>Conclusion</strong> The CRCgene database provides the context for genetic association data to be interpreted appropriately and helps inform future
                        research direction.
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
               </div><a href="/lookup/doi/10.1093/jnci/djs442"> ARTICLE</a><p id="p-5">Colorectal cancer is a major global public health problem, with approximately 950 000 patients newly diagnosed each year (<a href="http://www.who.int">www.who.int</a>; International Agency for Research on Cancer GLOBOCAN). The risk of developing colorectal cancer increases steeply with age
                  and its incidence is rising in many industrialized countries as life expectancy and the number of elderly people increase.
                  Incidence is also rising in many developing countries as diet and lifestyle change and increasingly resemble those in industrialized
                  countries. Overall, 5-year survival of colorectal cancer remains at best only approximately 50%. Because of the rising disease
                  burden, health care costs, including costs for diagnosis and treatment, are substantial.
               </p>
               <p id="p-6" class="indent">Approximately one-third of variance in colorectal cancer is attributed to inherited genetic factors (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>) and disease risk is increased by two- to fourfold for first-degree relatives of patients. Excess risk of familial cancer
                  can be accounted for by a combination of rare high-penetrance mutations and large numbers of common genetic variants, each
                  of which confers a small risk. These variants combine to confer a range of susceptibilities in the population (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>).
               </p>
               <p id="p-7" class="indent">The associations between genetic variants and human diseases described so far were highly dependent on the study designs used
                  to identify them. Highly penetrant mutations with large effects have been identified in a number of genes responsible for
                  heritable colorectal cancer susceptibility syndromes (eg, DNA mismatch repair genes, <em>APC</em>, <em>SMAD4</em>, <em>LKB1/STK11</em>, <em>MUTYH,</em> and a linkage region on 15q) and these mutations account for about 3% of incident colorectal cancers in industrialized countries.
                  Identification of further high-penetrance loci has proven intractable to genetic linkage approaches, probably because of locus
                  heterogeneity, and confounding, because of the segregation of multiple alleles in collected families. Association studies
                  conducted in general population samples using common genetic markers have typically identified variants with very small effects.
                  To date, genome-wide association studies (GWASs) have reported 14 common genetic variants that influence the risk of colorectal
                  cancer and account for approximately 6% of the excess familial risk (<a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2–10</a>). Future resequencing studies are expected to identify rarer variants (eg, with 0.05%–5% prevalence) with intermediate, or
                  perhaps even large, effects (<a id="xref-ref-2-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). GWASs of structural variations will likely identify deletions, amplifications, and other copy-number variations that may
                  also influence risk of colorectal cancer.
               </p>
               <p id="p-8" class="indent">For this report, we have undertaken a comprehensive review of genetic factors that appear to be associated with colorectal
                  cancer by using previously published guidelines for the assessment of cumulative evidence on genetic association studies (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>,<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>) and following a format similar to previous overview meta-analyses (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13–15</a>). We have cataloged all genetic association studies published in this field and conducted meta-analyses of variants with
                  genotypes available in four or more independent case-control studies; any variants that had been typed in two large GWASs
                  were also included in the meta-analyses. The results of the search strategy and meta-analyses are publicly available on a
                  regularly updated Internet database (CRCgene). This represents the first attempt to systematically capture all published genetic
                  association data for colorectal cancer and conduct a meta-analysis.
               </p>
               <p id="p-9" class="indent">The core aim is to provide an up-to-date systematic review of the state of the art across the field of colorectal cancer genetics
                  for the research community. We conducted a critical review of all published “candidate gene” study data, incorporating relevant
                  candidate gene data from the GWASs available to us, and then performed meta-analyses of these data. This approach enabled
                  us to summarize available evidence from larger sample sizes, thus gaining greater precision in odds ratio estimates. We have
                  presented these data within a defined statistical and causal inference framework to aid correct interpretation of data (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>).
               </p>
               <p id="p-10" class="indent">The broader medium-term aim of this work is to identify genetic variants for which there is robust evidence of influence on
                  risk of colorectal cancer. This will help to inform future research efforts and to identify variants that can serve as a basis
                  for providing risk estimates for population groups. It will also provide new insights into the fundamental biological mechanisms
                  involved in colorectal carcinogenesis.
               </p>
               <div class="section methods" id="sec-5">
                  <div class="section-nav"><a href="#abstract-1" title="Abstract" class="prev-section-link"><span>Previous Section</span></a><a href="#sec-11" title="Results" class="next-section-link"><span>Next Section</span></a></div>
                  
                  <h2>Methods</h2>
                  
                  <div id="sec-6" class="subsection">
                     
                     <h3>Literature Search and Data Collection</h3>
                     
                     <p id="p-11">The first step was to undertake a comprehensive systematic literature review of all current published data on genetics and
                        colorectal cancer. To identify gene association studies aimed at risk of colorectal cancer, we used the Medline database via
                        the Ovid gateway and the search terms comprising medical subject headings (MeSH) and keywords relating to colorectal neoplasms,
                        the MeSH heading “genetic predisposition to disease,” and the keywords “gene$” and “associate$” were applied to terms in the
                        entire article. We cross-checked these 10 125 findings against those listed in the HuGENet phenopedia (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>). Of the 10 145 articles thus identified, first we screened the abstracts for eligibility, and then, if necessary, the full
                        texts. We used the following inclusion and exclusion criteria. The article must have evaluated the association between a polymorphic
                        genetic variant [one with a minor allele frequency (MAF) ≥0.01 in the general population based on the data on the reference
                        panel of the 1000 GenomesTable 1; (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>)] and sporadic colorectal cancer. Studies that examined associations with only premalignant conditions such as adenomas,
                        polyps, or dysplastic tissue were not included. In addition, studies of hereditary colorectal cancer syndromes, such as familial
                        adenomatous polyposis, hereditary nonpolyposis colorectal cancer, juvenile polyposis syndrome, and Gardner’s syndrome were
                        excluded because our focus was on sporadic colorectal cancer. All studies needed to relate to human participants; any study
                        that was concerned solely with investigating the progression or histological phenotype of colorectal cancer was excluded.
                        Case–control studies and appropriate cohort and GWASs were included. The study had to be published in English in a peer review
                        journal before June 30, 2010. For variants that were identified through the GWAS, the search was repeated and extended until
                        March 31, 2011. Any research that had only been reported in abstracts (eg, presented in scientific conferences yet to be fully
                        published) was excluded. Nine family-based studies were also excluded. We generated a list with all variants to be summarized
                        using meta-analysis and compared it with a list of variants that were included in two GWASs, from Scotland and Canada, for
                        which we had access to individual-level data. The Scottish study comprised patients aged 16–79 years who were diagnosed with
                        colorectal cancer throughout Scotland during the period 1999–2006 (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>,<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>). Potentially eligible population-based control subjects were selected through the Community Health Index, a national register
                        of all individuals registered with a general practitioner in Scotland, and individually matched with patients on age (± 2
                        years), sex, and area of residence. The Canadian study was based on patients aged 20–74 years who were diagnosed during the
                        period July 1, 1997, to June 30, 2000, who were identified and recruited from the population-based Ontario Cancer Registry
                        (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>,<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>). Random digit dialing was used to select population-based control subjects who were frequency-matched with patients on sex
                        and 5-year age group. In both of these studies, DNA from blood was genotyped using multiple platforms; here, we included data
                        obtained in both studies from the Illumina HumanHap300 and HumanHap240S arrays on the Infinium platform. If a variant was
                        found to be included in either of these GWASs, then genotype counts were included in the meta-analysis of this variant. For
                        the other studies, we contacted the authors of studies with missing data, with an approximate successful response rate of
                        0.2.
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
                  <div id="sec-7" class="subsection">
                     
                     <h3>Data Entry, Management, and Abstraction</h3>
                     
                     <p id="p-12">Once the search was completed, the references of the articles in the search were entered into a web-based database, “RefWorks”
                        (<a href="http://www.refworks.com/">http://www.refworks.com/</a>), thus freezing the search at that point in time. Their abstracts and/or full texts were screened to assess their eligibility
                        for inclusion in the field synopsis. Review articles and meta-analyses on genetic associations of colorectal cancer were also
                        kept alongside so that the references they used could be screened for eligibility if they had been missed in the Medline search.
                     </p>
                     
                     <p id="p-13" class="indent">Data from all studies that met the final inclusion and exclusion criteria were abstracted into two standardized tables. The
                        first of these listed study characteristics and the second table listed allele and genotype frequencies. We abstracted key
                        variables with regard to the study identifiers and context, study design and limitations, intervention specifics, and outcome
                        effects.
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
                  <div id="sec-8" class="subsection">
                     
                     <h3>Statistical Analysis</h3>
                     
                     <p id="p-14">Statistical analysis was conducted using Intercooled STATA, version 11.0 (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>). Meta-analysis was performed for all variants with case-control data available from four or more independent samples. We
                        obtained summary crude odds ratios (ORs) and 95% confidence intervals (95% CI) for two additive models (variant [var]/wild-type
                        [wt] vs wt/wt; and var/var vs wt/wt), one recessive model (var/var vs var/wt and wt/wt), and one dominant model (var/var and
                        var/wt vs wt/wt). We applied either the fixed-effect model (Mantel–Haenszel method) or, in case of heterogeneity, the random-effect
                        model (DerSimonian–Laird method). Between-study heterogeneity was quantified by calculating the <em>Q</em> statistic, with a <em>P</em> value less than .05 being the threshold. We also calculated the <em>I</em>
                        <sup><em>2</em></sup> heterogeneity metric and its 95% confidence interval (CI). Although sometimes we summarized studies that were very heterogeneous,
                        we recognized that because of the variation in study methods and outcome definitions, the meta-estimates should be interpreted
                        cautiously. To assess any small-study effects, we performed funnel plot analyses and tested for statistical significance using
                        the Harbord modification of the Egger test, implemented in STATA (<a href="http://ideas.repec.org/a/tsj/stataj/v9y2009i2p197-210.html">http://ideas.repec.org/a/tsj/stataj/v9y2009i2p197-210.html</a>). A negative result from small study–effects testing does not entirely exclude publication bias. In addition, the test for
                        small-study effects may be underpowered if there are approximately 10 or fewer studies and it may be inappropriate in the
                        presence of large heterogeneity (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>). We also estimated the power that each meta-analysis had to detect a statistically significant effect by using the Power
                        and Sample Size Program (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>) and specifying α = .05 as the level of statistical significance and the effect sizes and allele frequencies estimated from
                        the meta-analyses (an integral component of the BFDP analysis).
                     </p>
                     
                     <p id="p-15" class="indent">The relative risk of colorectal cancer in a sibling that was attributable to a given single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP)
                        was calculated using the following formula (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>,<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>): 
                     </p>
                     <span class="disp-formula" id="disp-formula-1"><img class="graphic" alt="Formula" src="1433/embed/graphic-1.gif" />
                        
                        <span class="disp-formula-label">(1)</span></span>
                     
                     <p id="p-16">where <em>p</em> is the population frequency of the referent allele, <em>q</em> = 1– <em>p</em>, and <em>r</em><sub>1</sub> and <em>r</em><sub>2</sub> are the relative risks (estimated as ORs from the meta- analyses) for heterozygotes and variant homozygotes, relative to
                        wild-type homozygotes. Assuming a multiplicative interaction, we calculated the proportion of the familial risk attributable
                        to an SNP as log(λ*)/log(λ<sub>0</sub>), where λ<sub>0</sub> is the overall familial relative risk estimated from epidemiological studies, assumed to be 2.2. Although family studies
                        are normally used to estimate the relative risk for siblings, this formula can be used in population-based studies (<a id="xref-ref-24-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>,<a id="xref-ref-25-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>).
                     </p>
                     
                     <p id="p-17" class="indent">Finally, we repeated the analysis by excluding any studies that were conducted in a nonwhite population (with white populations
                        defined as Europeans, North Americans, and Australians) for the SNPs that were found to be statistically significantly associated
                        with risk of colorectal cancer in any of the genetic models at a threshold level of α = .05.
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
                  <div id="sec-9" class="subsection">
                     
                     <h3>Credibility of Genetic Association</h3>
                     
                     <p id="p-18">To assess the credibility of genetic associations, we considered the BFDP (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>) and the Venice criteria (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>,<a id="xref-ref-12-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>). The BFDP assesses the noteworthiness of an observed association. For variants that were found to be statistically significantly
                        associated with risk of colorectal cancer in any of the genetic models (at <em>P</em> &lt; .05), the BFDP was estimated using the Excel Calculation Spreadsheet (<a href="http://faculty.washington.edu/jonno/cv.html">http://faculty.washington.edu/jonno/cv.html</a>). The BFDP threshold for noteworthiness was set up to be equal to 0.20, based on the assumption that a false discovery would
                        be four times more costly than a false nondiscovery. We chose to calculate BFDP values for two levels of prior probabilities:
                        at a medium or low prior level (0.05 to 10<sup>–3</sup>) that would be close to what would be expected for a candidate gene; and at a very low prior level (10<sup>–4</sup> to 10<sup>–6</sup>) that would be close to what would be expected for a random SNP.
                     </p>
                     
                     <p id="p-19" class="indent">With regard to the Venice criteria, we operationalized the criterion of volume of evidence on the basis of statistical power
                        to detect an association of the desired magnitude: A, 80% or more; B, 50%–79%; or C, less than 50%. For replication, we used
                        the <em>I</em>² criterion proposed by Ioannidis et al. (<a id="xref-ref-11-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). For protection against bias, we considered that complete reporting was problematic. The phenotype definition was addressed
                        by our inclusion criterion ––namely, that case subjects would have newly incident colorectal cancer. In general, genotyping
                        error rates are low (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>), and the criterion of replication across studies in part addressed potential concern about variation in genotyping quality
                        between studies; some genotyping issues are discussed in relation to specific findings below. Whereas population stratification
                        may impact gene discovery (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>), the effect on the magnitude of association in general appears to be small (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>,<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>).
                     </p>
                     
                     <p id="p-20" class="indent">We classified the genetic associations in three categories according to the findings after the BFDP analysis and the application
                        of the Venice criteria. Associations were classified as positive if they fulfilled the following criteria: 1) they were statistically
                        significant (at <em>P</em> &lt; .05) in at least two of the genetic models; 2) they had a BFDP less than 0.20 (at least when P &lt; .05); 3) they had a statistical
                        power greater than 80%; and 4) they had an <em>I</em>
                        <sup>2</sup> less than 50%. Associations were classified as less-credible positives if they were statistically significant (<em>P</em> &lt; .05) in at least one of the genetic models, their BFDP was greater than 0.20, and their statistical power was between 50%
                        and 79% (<em>I</em>
                        <sup>2</sup> ranged from 0% to 89% , but this criterion was not taken into account for this category). All other associations were classified
                        as negative.
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
                  <div id="sec-10" class="subsection">
                     
                     <h3>Model-free Approach</h3>
                     
                     <p id="p-21">For those SNPs that were identified as true positives after applying the BFDP and Venice criteria, we applied the model-free
                        meta-analysis approach described by Thompson et al. (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). This model uses a maximum likelihood estimator, assumes a fixed-effect meta-analysis and is similar to a bivariate meta-analysis
                        of the effects for the heterozygotes and variant homozygotes. It gives an estimate of the lambda (λ), which represents the
                        underlying genetic model (and it represents the heterozygote effect as a proportion of the homozygote effect), with its confidence
                        interval limits. If the confidence interval of λ is too wide, there is not enough power to estimate λ. The value of λ is not
                        restricted. Values equal to 0, 0.5, and 1.0 correspond to the recessive, additive, and dominant genetic models, respectively.
                        Values greater than 1 or smaller than 0 suggest heterosis, which is when the risk of the heterozygotes is higher or lower,
                        respectively, than the risk of either of the homozygote genotypes. However, heterosis is relatively uncommon and λ values
                        greater than 1 or smaller than 0 could represent chance fluctuation, reflecting low statistical power to estimate the magnitude
                        of effect for heterozygotes.
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
               </div>
               <div class="section" id="sec-11">
                  <div class="section-nav"><a href="#sec-5" title="Methods" class="prev-section-link"><span>Previous Section</span></a><a href="#sec-14" title="Discussion" class="next-section-link"><span>Next Section</span></a></div>
                  
                  <h2>Results</h2>
                  
                  <div id="sec-12" class="subsection">
                     
                     <h3>Literature Search and Data Collection</h3>
                     
                     <p id="p-22">After screening approximately 10 145 titles and abstracts (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 1</a>, available online) dated until June 30, 2010 (or March 31, 2011 for GWAS-identified hits), we identified and extracted data
                        from 635 publications reporting on 445 polymorphisms in 110 different genes. More than 9231 (88%) of these studies were published
                        after 1995, and about half of those were published during the past 3 years.
                     </p>
                     
                     <div class="table pos-float" id="T1">
                        <div class="table-inline">
                           <div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links">
                                 <li><a href="1433/T1.expansion.html">In this window</a></li>
                                 <li><a class="in-nw" href="1433/T1.expansion.html">In a new window</a></li>
                              </ul>
                           </div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> 
                           
                           <p id="p-23" class="first-child">List of genes and variants that were selected for meta-analysis (sorted by gene pathway)*</p>
                           
                           <div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>
                        </div>
                     </div>
                     
                  </div>
                  
                  <div id="sec-13" class="subsection">
                     
                     <h3>Statistical Analysis</h3>
                     
                     <p id="p-38">Meta-analyses were conducted for 92 polymorphisms in 64 different loci (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>) with genotypes available in four or more candidate or GWA studies. On average, these meta-analyses were based on data from
                        5281 case patients (median interquartile range = 13 472 – 3384) and 6594 control subjects (median interquartile range = 16 102
                        – 4534) originating from eight (median interquartile range = 13 – 6) case–control samples. Unpublished data from a Scottish
                        and/or a Canadian GWAS were included in the analyses of 37 SNPs. Summary crude odds ratios (ORs) and 95% confidence intervals
                        for two additive models (var/wt vs wt/wt and var/var vs wt/wt) for variants that were identified from candidate studies are
                        presented in <a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a> and for variants that were identified from GWAS, in <a id="xref-table-wrap-3-1" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table 3</a>. Summary crude odds ratios and 95% confidence intervals for a recessive (var/var vs var/wt and wt/wt) and a dominant model
                        (var/var and var/wt vs wt/wt) are presented in <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a> (available online), for variants that were identified from candidate studies, and <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 3</a> (available online), for variants that were identified from GWAS. For 37 associations in which the 95% confidence intervals
                        excluded unity, we repeated the analysis excluding studies in nonwhite populations (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 4 and 5</a>, available online). We checked the linkage disequilibrium (LD) for all the polymorphisms that were included in our study
                        and we found that the following variants were in LD (<em>r</em><sup>2</sup> threshold ≥ 0.60): 1) rs4939827 (in <em>SMAD7</em>) and rs12953717 (in <em>SMAD7, r</em><sup>2</sup> = 0.60); 2) rs3813867 (in <em>CYP2E1</em>) and rs2031920 (in <em>CYP2E1, r</em><sup>2</sup> = 0.87); 3) rs10505477 (in <em>8q24</em>) and rs6983267 (in <em>8q24, r</em><sup>2</sup> = 0.88); 4) rs731236 (in <em>VDR</em>) and rs1544410 (in <em>VDR, r</em><sup>2</sup> = 1.00).
                     </p>
                     
                     <div class="table pos-float" id="T2">
                        <div class="table-inline">
                           <div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links">
                                 <li><a href="1433/T2.expansion.html">In this window</a></li>
                                 <li><a class="in-nw" href="1433/T2.expansion.html">In a new window</a></li>
                              </ul>
                           </div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 2. </span> 
                           
                           <p id="p-39" class="first-child">Summary crude odds ratios (ORs) and 95% confidence intervals (95% CIs) for two additive models for variants that were identified
                              from candidate studies
                           </p>
                           
                           <div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>
                        </div>
                     </div>
                     
                     <div class="table pos-float" id="T3">
                        <div class="table-inline">
                           <div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links">
                                 <li><a href="1433/T3.expansion.html">In this window</a></li>
                                 <li><a class="in-nw" href="1433/T3.expansion.html">In a new window</a></li>
                              </ul>
                           </div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 3. </span> 
                           
                           <p id="p-51" class="first-child">Summary crude odds ratios (ORs) and 95% confidence intervals (95% CIs) for two additive models for variants that were identified
                              from genome-wide association studies
                           </p>
                           
                           <div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>
                        </div>
                     </div>
                     
                     <p id="p-59" class="indent">To assess the credibility of genetic associations, we considered the Venice criteria (<a id="xref-ref-11-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>,<a id="xref-ref-12-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>) and the BFDP (<a id="xref-ref-26-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>) (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 6</a>, available online). Applying these filters indicate that associations with 16 variants (17% of the meta-analyzed SNPs) tagging
                        13 loci (<em>SMAD7, MUTYH, MTHFR,</em> and the 8q24, 8q23.3, 11q23.1, 14q22.2, 1q41, 20p12.3, 20q13.33, 3q26.2, 16q22.1, and 19q13.1) represent the most credible
                        findings and these will be referred as “positive” SNP associations (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-2" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Tables 2</a> and <a id="xref-table-wrap-3-2" class="xref-table" href="#T3">3</a>; <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 2 and 3</a> and <a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">Supplementary Figures 1–64</a>, available on the CRCgene website). These findings are based on accrued data on 16 000–40 604 case patients and on 15 216–42 672
                        control subjects, with a median of 1616 case patients per study. All variants that were included in the “positive” SNP associations
                        reached genome-wide statistical significance (<em>P</em> ≤ 1.7 × 10<sup>–7</sup>) in at least one meta-analysis model, apart from the ones in <em>MUTYH</em> and  in 19q13.1.
                     </p>
                     
                     <p id="p-60" class="indent">We identified “less-credible positive” associations (higher heterogeneity, lower statistical power, BFDP &gt; 0.2) with 23 variants
                        (25% of the meta-analyzed SNPs) of 22 loci (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-3" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Tables 2</a> and <a id="xref-table-wrap-3-3" class="xref-table" href="#T3">3</a>; <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 2 and 3</a> and <a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">Supplementary Figures 65–146</a>, available on the CRCgene website. These findings were based on accrued data on 857–26 370 case patients and on 932–26 662
                        control subjects, with a median of at least 600 case patients per study. Variants in the 10p14 and 15q14 loci reached genome-wide
                        statistical significance (<em>P</em> ≤ 1.7 × 10<sup>–7</sup>) in at least one meta-analysis model.
                     </p>
                     
                     <p id="p-61" class="indent">For those SNPs that were identified as “positives” or “less-credible positives” after applying the BFDP and Venice criteria,
                        we applied the model-free meta-analysis approach as described by Thompson et al. (<a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>) (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 7</a>, available online), which gives an estimate, <em>λ,</em> of the underlying genetic model. Funnel plots were produced for all the positive and less-credible associations. There was
                        no evidence of small-study effects, apart from the associations with rs1799750 (<em>MMP1</em>), rs34743033 (<em>TS</em>), <em>GSTM1</em> deletion, rs36053993 (<em>MUTYH</em>), rs10411210 (19q13.1), rs10936599 (3q26.2). However, only the <em>GSTM1</em> deletion and rs10411210 (19q13.1) and rs10936599 (3q26.2) tests were based on more than 10 studies and satisfied the conditions
                        of no heterogeneity. Therefore, the results of the other SNPs, for which there was some evidence of a small-study effect,
                        should be interpreted with caution. The remaining 53 (58%) meta-analyzed SNPs of 37 loci are designated as “negatives,” based
                        on accrued data from 575 to 17 178 case patients and 836 to 24 792 control subjects, with an average of at least 600 case
                        patients per study.
                     </p>
                     
                  </div>
                  
               </div>
               <div class="section" id="sec-14">
                  <div class="section-nav"><a href="#sec-11" title="Results" class="prev-section-link"><span>Previous Section</span></a><a href="#sec-15" title="Funding" class="next-section-link"><span>Next Section</span></a></div>
                  
                  <h2>Discussion</h2>
                  
                  <p id="p-62">This is the first systematic, comprehensive field synopsis of genetic association studies on colorectal cancer to our knowledge.
                     It differs from the recently published database of cancer genetic association (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>) in that it has collated and extracted data from more than 600 publications on more than 400 polymorphisms in 110 different
                     genes and presents the results of more than 90 new meta-analyses, rather than acting as a portal to already published meta-analyses
                     and GWASs. Furthermore, we have classified the results of our analysis as “positive associations,” “less-credible positive
                     associations,” and “negative associations” within a defined statistical and causal inference framework, including the Venice
                     criteria (<a id="xref-ref-11-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>,<a id="xref-ref-12-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>) the BDFP, and the model-free meta-analysis approach.
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-63" class="indent">Two SNPs at the 8q24 locus (rs6983267 and rs10505477) identified in the GWASs had a large volume of evidence and showed positive
                     associations irrespective of the genetic model considered, with moderate heterogeneity, and the model-free approach suggested
                     an underlying additive model (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-4" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table 3</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 3 and 7</a>). The SNP rs6983267 may be a somatic target in colorectal cancer (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>) and may be associated with enhanced responsiveness to Wnt signaling (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>). Further, rs6983267 has also been found to be associated with other types of cancer (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>,<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>), including prostate cancer (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38–40</a>). Interaction with the <em>MYC</em> proto-oncogene has been controversial (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41–45</a>), but recently in functional studies in cell lines, interaction between enhancer elements in the 8q24 locus and the MYC promoter,
                     via transcription factor Tcf-4 binding and allele-specific regulation of MYC expression, has been demonstrated (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-64" class="indent">One variant close to <em>SMAD7</em> (rs4939827), which was inversely associated with colorectal cancer, and two variants (rs12953717 and rs4464148), which were
                     associated with increased risk for the disease, were again identified through GWASs with large numbers of participants. The
                     associations were apparent irrespective of the genetic model, with moderate heterogeneity, and the model-free approach suggested
                     an underlying additive model (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-5" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table 3</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 3 and 7</a>). The SMAD7 protein is an inhibitor for the TGFβ signaling pathway (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>). The otherwise-tentative associations with the <em>TGFβ1</em> rs1800469 (C5097) and <em>TGFβR1</em> rs11466445 (*6A) variants may be consistent with this finding.
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-65" class="indent">Dysfunction of base-excision repair, the major pathway for repairing oxidative damage, has been implicated in the development
                     of multiple colorectal adenomas and colorectal cancer (MUTYH-associated polyposis [MAP] syndrome). Two variants of <em>MUTYH</em> were associated with increased risk of colorectal cancer, with little heterogeneity apparent (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-4" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>), providing further evidence that the single variants can act as lower-penetrance risk alleles of smaller effect, validating
                     the recent results for Y179C (rs34612342) (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>) and also highlighting the possible role of G396D (rs36053993). The BFDP was substantially less for rs36053993 than for rs34612342,
                     possibly because of the very low MAF of the rs34612342 variant, and therefore, rs36053993 was classified as having a positive
                     association, whereas rs34612342 was classified as having a less-credible positive association with colorectal cancer (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Although there was formally statistically significant evidence of small-study effects for rs36053993 (<a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">Supplementary Figures 189–190</a>, available on the CRCgene website), the magnitude of this effect was small and has to be interpreted in the context of multiple
                     tests for small-study effects. The model-free approach suggested a recessive model for rs36053993 and an additive (but with
                     wide CI) for rs34612342 (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 7</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-66" class="indent">An inverse association with homozygosity for the <em>MTHFR</em> rs1801133 (commonly reported as C677T) variant was observed in 52 studies comparing more than 27 000 case patients and almost
                     40 000 control subjects, with moderate heterogeneity and an underlying recessive model as suggested by the model-free approach
                     (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-5" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 2 and 7</a>). A similar association was observed in a smaller body of evidence with the rs1801131 (commonly known as A1298C) variant,
                     with little heterogeneity between 34 studies (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-6" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 4</a>). In most populations, strong LD between the variants has been observed (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>,<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>). These findings extend and concur with previous systematic reviews and meta-analyses (<a id="xref-ref-51-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51–56</a>). All of the studies considered these <em>MTHFR</em> variants as candidates for association with colorectal cancer on the basis of knowledge about their effects on enzyme function
                     and associations with blood levels of folate and related metabolites. However, the evidence is further reinforced by GWASs
                     of plasma homocysteine confirming some of the earlier work on the effects of the <em>MTHFR</em> C677T variant (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57–60</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-67" class="indent">The other variants with which associations were observed were all based on accumulated data on a total of at least 17 000
                     case patients and 18 000 control subjects, with a median of 1506 case patients per study. Positive associations with colorectal
                     cancer were identified with variants at the 8q23.3 (rs16892766), 11q23.1 (rs3802842), 14q22.2 (rs4444235), 1q41 (rs6691170),
                     and 20p12.3 (rs961253) loci, and inverse associations were identified with variants at the 20q13.33 (rs4925386), 3q26.2 (rs10936599),
                     16q22.1 (rs9929218), and 19q13.1 (rs10411210) loci. Although there was formally statistically significant evidence of small-study
                     effects for 3q26.2 (rs10936599) (<a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">Supplementary Figures 209–210</a>, available on the CRCgene website) and 19q13.1 (rs10411210; <a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">Supplementary Figures 203–204</a>), the magnitude of these effects was not large, and the findings of GWASs are less prone to the problem of selective reporting
                     than studies that target only a few risk factors at a time with only selected findings being published (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">61</a>). In a study of somatic genetic changes, no allelic imbalance targeting 11q23.1 (rs3802842), 14q22.2 (rs4444235), 16q22.1
                     (rs9929218), 19q13.1 (rs10411210), or 20p12.3 (rs961253) was observed (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-6" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table 3</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 3</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">62</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-68" class="indent">The less-credible positive associations with 23 variants of 22 genes involved the following pathways—adhesion (<em>CDH1, MMP1,</em> and <em>MMP3</em>); alcohol metabolism (<em>ALDH2</em>); base-excision repair (<em>XRCC1</em> and <em>MUTYH</em>); inflammation and immune response (<em>IL6</em>, two variants of <em>NOD2</em>); inhibition of cell growth (<em>CCND1, TGFB1,</em> and <em>TGFBR1</em>); one-carbon metabolism (<em>TS</em>); substrate metabolism (<em>CYP1A1, GSTM1, GSTT1,</em> and <em>NAT1</em>); vitamin D metabolism (<em>VDR</em>)—common low-penetrance variants at 9p24 (rs719725), 10p14 (rs10795668), 15q14 (rs4779584), and 12q13.13 (rs11169552); and
                     the common rs459552 (D1822V) variant of <em>APC</em>, for which rare variants confer a high risk of colorectal cancer. Some of these have been considered in previous meta-analyses.
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-69" class="indent">The association with the <em>CDH1</em> rs16260 (C160A) variant remained apparent when the analysis was restricted to populations of European origin (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 4 and 5</a>). No association was apparent in an earlier meta-analysis by Wang et al. (<a id="xref-ref-63-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">63</a>), but this meta-analysis included less than one-tenth of the number of case and control subjects included in the present
                     study. Previous meta-analyses have found positive associations with the <em>MMP1</em> rs1799750 (G-1607GG) but not the <em>MMP3</em> rs3025058 (AAAAA 1612 AAAAAA) variant (<a id="xref-ref-64-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">64</a>,<a id="xref-ref-65-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">65</a>). We found evidence of small-study effects for <em>MMP1</em> rs1799750 (<a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">Supplementary Figures 149–150</a>, available on the CRCgene website.
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-70" class="indent">With regard to <em>ALDH2</em>, our findings are similar to those of Wang et al. (<a id="xref-ref-66-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">66</a>). All of these studies were carried out in Asian populations, in which the rs671 (Glu487Lys) variant is more common than
                     in the other populations (MAF is 0 in white, 0.16 in Chinese, and 0.23 in Japanese populations). The association between the
                     <em>ALDH2</em> variant and colorectal adenomas has been investigated, but it has been interpreted as null (<a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">67</a>,<a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">68</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-71" class="indent">Inverse associations were observed with the <em>XRCC1</em> rs25487 (Arg399Gln) variant in the additive and recessive models with heterogeneity, which persisted when the analysis was
                     restricted to populations of European origin (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-7" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 2, 4, and 5</a>). An earlier meta-analysis with about half the number of participants also detected an inverse association for the recessive
                     model (<a id="xref-ref-69-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">69</a>), but another meta-analysis that included a smaller number of participants (but more studies) was interpreted as null (<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>). It has been suggested that the association between this <em>XRCC1</em> variant and colorectal adenomas is stronger than that apparent with colorectal cancer (<a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">71</a>), but we note that the volume of evidence relating to adenomas is substantially smaller. Three variants of NOD1 (rs5743293,
                     rs2066845, and rs2066844) are associated with increased risk when a dominant model is assumed (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>); this finding is consistent with that of Tian et al. (<a id="xref-ref-72-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">72</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-72" class="indent">Assuming a dominant model, variants at three gene loci that are involved in the inhibition of cell growth are associated with
                     an increased risk of colorectal cancer. First, the analysis of the <em>CCND1</em> rs17852153 (G870A) variant includes approximately five times as many participants as the earlier study of Tan et al. (<a id="xref-ref-73-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">73</a>) (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 4</a>). In the study of Tan et al., there was a concern about small-study effects, but we found no evidence of this problem (<a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">Supplementary Figures 165–166</a>). Two studies of colorectal adenomas give inconsistent results (<a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">74</a>,<a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">75</a>). Second, the <em>TGFB1</em> rs1800469 (C5097) variant has been investigated solely in Asian populations (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-76-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">76</a>). Third, previous meta-analyses have drawn different conclusions about the association with the <em>TGFBR1</em> rs11466445 (*6A) variant (<a id="xref-ref-77-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">77–79</a>). The magnitude of association in the present meta-analysis is similar to that determined in previous reports (<a id="xref-ref-78-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">78</a>,<a id="xref-ref-79-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">79</a>), but the inclusion of data on an additional 2000 participants from two studies has increased statistical power but not heterogeneity
                     (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-73" class="indent">Our finding of an inverse association with the <em>TS</em> rs34743033 (TSER) variant assuming either an additive or dominant model remained apparent when the analysis was restricted
                     to populations of European origin (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-8" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 2, 4, and 5</a>), although there was evidence of small-study effects for the comparison between homozygotes under the additive model. Two
                     studies of colorectal adenomas do not suggest an overall association with this variant (<a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">80</a>,<a id="xref-ref-81-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">81</a>), and one study did not detect an association with adenoma recurrence (<a id="xref-ref-82-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-82">82</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-74" class="indent">There has been a longstanding interest in the possible effects of genetic variants influencing the metabolism of diverse substrates,
                     including potential carcinogens, on cancer risk. Cytochrome P-450 CYP1A1 is involved in the metabolism of polycyclic aromatic
                     hydrocarbons and estrogens, and perhaps cruciferous vegetables and heterocyclic amines (<a id="xref-ref-83-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">83</a>). Further, CYP1A1 mRNA has been detected in the large bowel (<a id="xref-ref-84-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-84">84</a>,<a id="xref-ref-85-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">85</a>), and human colon cell lines also express CYP1A1 at the protein and mRNA levels (<a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">86</a>). These observations stimulated many candidate gene studies of the association between colorectal cancer and <em>CYP1A1</em> variants. The positive association with presence of the rs1048943 (A2454G, Ile462Val) variant apparent in the present analysis
                     is in line with the results of a smaller recent meta-analysis (<a id="xref-ref-87-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">87</a>), whereas an earlier meta-analysis (<a id="xref-ref-88-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">88</a>) did not detect this association (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-9" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>). No association was apparent with the rs4646903 variant, but there was low statistical power to detect this association
                     (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-10" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-75" class="indent">Another intensively investigated gene polymorphism that is thought to affect the metabolism of multiple substrates is the
                     <em>GSTM1</em> null variant, with which we observed a weak positive association in data from more than 18 000 case patients and more than
                     26 000 control subjects from 43 studies (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>), with some evidence of small-study effects. The weak positive association was also apparent when analysis was restricted
                     to 25 studies in populations of European origin (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 5</a>). There have been several previous meta-analyses; the earliest of these studies indicated no overall association (<a id="xref-ref-88-11" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">88–90</a>), whereas the more recent studies showed a positive association (<a id="xref-ref-91-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">91–93</a>). No clear association between <em>GSTM1</em> and adenomas has been observed (<a id="xref-ref-94-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-94">94</a>). In data from more than 13 000 case patients and 20 000 control subjects from 35 studies, there was also a positive association
                     with the <em>GSTT1</em> null deletion, in line with previous meta-analyses (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-88-12" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">88</a>,<a id="xref-ref-92-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-92">92</a>,<a id="xref-ref-95-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">95</a>,<a id="xref-ref-96-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-96">96</a>). The positive association remained apparent when analysis was restricted to approximately half the number of participants
                     from 20 studies in populations of European origin (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Studies of <em>GSTT1</em> and adenomas have not detected a clear association (<a id="xref-ref-94-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-94">94</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-76" class="indent">In data from nearly 5000 case patients and more than 6000 control subjects from 15 studies, there was a positive association
                     with heterogeneity for <em>NAT1</em> alleles classified as being associated with rapid acetylation (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-11" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>). No association was apparent with homozygosity for alleles associated with rapid acetylation or assuming other models of
                     inheritance. Furthermore, the association was not apparent when analysis was restricted to populations of European origin
                     (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 4 and 5</a>). A previous meta-analysis found no association with carriage of the <em>NAT1*10</em> variant in less than 1000 participants from three studies (<a id="xref-ref-88-13" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">88</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-77" class="indent">There was an inverse association between colorectal cancer and the <em>VDR</em> rs1544410 (Bsml 6089GA) variant, but with considerable heterogeneity between studies (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-12" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>). This heterogeneity was reduced when analysis was limited to populations of European origin (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Tables 4 and 5</a>). No association between this variant and colorectal adenomas (<a id="xref-ref-97-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-97">97–99</a>) or recurrence (<a id="xref-ref-100-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-100">100</a>,<a id="xref-ref-101-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-101">101</a>) has been observed.
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-78" class="indent">On the basis of GWASs, a positive association with the rs719725 variant at 9p24 was observed with moderate heterogeneity in
                     data from more than 11 000 case patients and 13 000 control subjects from 13 studies. This finding is consistent with the
                     recent meta-analysis of Kocarnick et al. (<a id="xref-ref-102-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-102">102</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-79" class="indent">Assuming a recessive mode of inheritance, an inverse association was found with the rs10795668 at 10p14, based on data from
                     more than 20 000 case patients and more than 20 000 control subjects from six studies (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-7" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table 3</a>). The magnitude of association was attenuated when the analysis was restricted to populations of European origin (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 4</a>). In a study of somatic genetic changes, no allelic imbalance targeting this SNP was observed (<a id="xref-ref-62-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">62</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-80" class="indent">We considered the associations with one variant at 15q14 (rs4779584) and one at 12q13.13 (rs11169552) to lie in the less-credible
                     category of associations because of heterogeneity between studies. Based on data from more than 13 500 case patients and 12 500
                     control subjects from nine studies, there was a positive association with the rs4779584 variant (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-8" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table 3</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 3</a>), and based on data from nearly 18 000 case patients and nearly 20 000 control subjects from 11 studies, an inverse association
                     with the rs11169552 variant (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-9" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table 3</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 3</a>). In a study of somatic genetic changes, no allelic imbalance targeting the former variant was observed (<a id="xref-ref-62-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">62</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-81" class="indent">On the basis of studies carried out solely in populations of European origin, homozygosity for the common rs459552 (D1822V)
                     variant of <em>APC,</em> rare truncating germline variants of which cause familial adenomatous polyposis (FAP), was inversely associated with colorectal
                     cancer (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 4</a>). No marginal association between this variant and colorectal adenomas (<a id="xref-ref-103-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-103">103</a>,<a id="xref-ref-104-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-104">104</a>) or advanced adenomas (ie, ≥1cm or containing villous elements or high-grade dysplasia) of the distal colon (<a id="xref-ref-105-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-105">105</a>) has been observed.
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-82" class="indent">For variants for which we did not find associations in our meta-analyses, the accumulated number of case patients ranged between
                     nearly 600 and slightly more than 15 000, with 20 variants in which the accumulated number of case patients exceeded 5000
                     (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-13" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>, <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/content/suppl/2012/09/16/djs369.DC2">Supplementary Table 2</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-83" class="indent">The lack of association with the <em>PPARγ</em> rs1801282 variant does not support an earlier meta-analysis (<a id="xref-ref-106-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-106">106</a>), whereas the lack of association with the <em>PPARγ</em> rs3856606 variant is consistent with the results of Lu et al. (<a id="xref-ref-107-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-107">107</a>). Earlier work on <em>NAT2</em> suggested a positive association with the rapid acetylator phenotype, but no association with acetylator status could be
                     inferred from genotype (<a id="xref-ref-51-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>,<a id="xref-ref-88-14" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">88</a>,<a id="xref-ref-108-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-108">108</a>,<a id="xref-ref-109-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-109">109</a>). No overall association was apparent in an earlier meta-analysis with homozygosity for the <em>MTR</em> rs1805087 (A2576G) variant, based on nine studies (<a id="xref-ref-110-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-110">110</a>). The current analysis approximately doubles the data on colorectal cancer and the <em>GSTP1</em> rs1695 (Ile105Val) variant. As in the meta-analysis of Economoupoulos and Sergentanis (<a id="xref-ref-92-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-92">92</a>), it does not confirm an earlier analysis that found an inverse association when a recessive model was assumed (<a id="xref-ref-111-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-111">111</a>). Studies of colorectal adenomas do not suggest any association with this <em>GSTP1</em> variant, although subgroup effects have been reported (<a id="xref-ref-112-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-112">112–115</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-84" class="indent">The results presented here are in accordance with previous meta-analyses that did not show any association with the <em>IGF1</em> CA-repeat polymorphism (<a id="xref-ref-116-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-116">116</a>,<a id="xref-ref-117-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-117">117</a>), although one of these showed an association with a higher serum concentration of IGF1 (<a id="xref-ref-117-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-117">117</a>). Our finding of no association with the <em>IGFBP3</em> rs2854744 (A202C) variant is consistent with that reported by Chen (<a id="xref-ref-117-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-117">117</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-85" class="indent">There have been numerous studies assessing the possible relationship between common variants of the <em>TP53</em> tumor suppressor gene and several cancer types. The relationship between colorectal cancer and the rs1042522 (codon 72) variant
                     has been investigated in previous meta-analyses, none of which suggest an association across multiple small studies, although
                     marked heterogeneity was observed (<a id="xref-ref-118-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-118">118–121</a>). Of note, one of these highlights the differences in results according to genotyping method and source of tissue for genotyping
                     case patients (<a id="xref-ref-118-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-118">118</a>). With regard to the <em>APC</em> rs1801166 (E1317Q) variant, one previous analysis of colorectal cancer was interpreted as indicating no association (<a id="xref-ref-122-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-122">122</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-86" class="indent">The current analysis examines more than double the amount of genetic association data for the <em>XRCC1</em> variant (Arg194Trp) and is consistent with previous meta-analyses indicating no association (<a id="xref-ref-69-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">69</a>,<a id="xref-ref-70-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-87" class="indent">Because of the inverse association between aspirin and colorectal neoplasia, many investigations have been carried out on
                     genetic variants that might influence cyclooxygenase (COX) metabolism. We did not find any association with <em>PTGS2/COX2</em> variants: neither with the rs20417 (G765C) variant (for which there is most evidence), in accordance with Cao (<a id="xref-ref-123-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-123">123</a>), nor with the other variants. Individually, adenoma studies did not suggest associations for newly detected adenomas with
                     the rs20417 (<a id="xref-ref-124-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-124">124–127</a>), the rs5275 (<a id="xref-ref-124-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-124">124</a>,<a id="xref-ref-125-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-125">125</a>), the rs5277 (<a id="xref-ref-124-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-124">124</a>,<a id="xref-ref-125-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-125">125</a>), or the rs689466 variants (<a id="xref-ref-125-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-125">125</a>,<a id="xref-ref-126-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-126">126</a>). With regard to increased risk of adenoma recurrence, individual studies found no associations with the rs20417 (<a id="xref-ref-128-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-128">128</a>,<a id="xref-ref-129-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-129">129</a>) or the rs5275 variants (<a id="xref-ref-129-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-129">129</a>), but one study reported an increased risk associated with homozygosity for the uncommon variant of rs5277 (<a id="xref-ref-129-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-129">129</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-88" class="indent">The lack of association with the <em>NQ01</em> rs1800566 variant was also found in an earlier meta-analysis based on about one-third the number of subjects (<a id="xref-ref-130-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-130">130</a>). With regard to the other variants for which we conducted meta-analyses, our finding of no association is consistent with
                     earlier meta-analyses for <em>MMP9</em> (<a id="xref-ref-64-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">64</a>), <em>VEGF</em> rs2010963 (G634C) (<a id="xref-ref-131-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-131">131</a>), <em>XRCC1</em> rs25489 (Arg280His) (<a id="xref-ref-70-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>), <em>XRCC3</em> rs861359 (Thr241Met) (<a id="xref-ref-69-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">69</a>), <em>TNFα</em> rs1800629 (308G&gt;A) (<a id="xref-ref-132-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-132">132</a>), and <em>CYP2E1</em> rs3813867 (Pstl/Rsal, 1293G&gt;C) (<a id="xref-ref-133-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-133">133</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-89" class="indent">We were not able to identify previous meta-analyses of <em>ADH1B</em> rs1229984, <em>ADH1C</em> rs698, <em>VEGF</em> rs3025039, <em>OGG1</em> rs1052133, <em>IL8</em> rs4073, <em>IL10</em> rs1800896, <em>APOE</em> rs429358, <em>TS</em> rs34489327, <em>CYP1A1</em> rs4646903, <em>CYP1A2</em> rs762551, <em>CYP2E1</em> rs2031920, <em>GSTP1</em> rs1138272, <em>VDR</em> rs731236 <em>or the MLH1</em> rs1799977, and rs1800734 variants.
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-90" class="indent">The substantial majority of studies are from Western Europe, North America, and parts of Asia (more than one study from China,
                     Japan, Korea, Singapore, and Taiwan). Investigation of more diverse populations is important, as it will enable variants to
                     be considered in populations with more diverse patterns of LD and gene–environment interaction.
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-91" class="indent">The number of common, low-penetrance variants that appear to be associated with colorectal cancer is very much less than anticipated,
                     therefore decreasing the feasibility of combining variants as a profile in a prediction tool for stratifying screening modalities
                     or primary prevention approaches (<a id="xref-ref-134-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-134">134</a>). In addition, the variants so far identified account for only a small proportion of the familial risk (6.1% for “positive”
                     and 14.1% when adding the “less-credible positive” variants; <a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). As for other common diseases, there is increasing interest in the effects of lower-frequency SNPs and rare variants, types
                     of genetic variation other than SNPs, and the effects of possible gene–environment and gene–gene interactions. Further, advances
                     in technology enable the capture of large amounts of data on types of biological variations, in addition to genomic, including
                     epigenetic, proteomic, transcriptomic, and metabolomic variations. These developments make this field synopsis the beginning
                     of a work in progress, and we plan regular searches of the literature using the methodology described here and will continuously
                     update the website when new data are available (<a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/</a>).
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-92" class="indent">There is an overlap of some of these loci and variants with the loci of genetic risk factors for other common complex diseases
                     and disease traits (<a id="xref-ref-135-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-135">135</a>). In particular, several of these genes show genome-wide associations (<em>P</em> &lt; 5 × 10<sup>–8</sup>; replicated in at least one other study) with other cancers: breast cancer (<em>CCND1</em>), prostate cancer (8q24), and bladder cancer (<em>GSTM1</em>); or other diseases or disease traits: mean corpuscular hemoglobin concentration and alanine aminotransferase levels (<em>ALDH2</em>), ulcerative colitis (<em>CDH1</em>), diastolic blood pressure (<em>CYP1A1</em>), CRP (<em>IL6</em>), serum matrix metalloproteinase (<em>MMP1</em> and <em>MMP3</em>), systolic blood pressure, and plasma homocysteine (<em>MTHFR</em>), Crohn’s disease and inflammatory bowel disease (<em>NOD2</em>). The pleiotropic associations with 8q24, <em>MTHFR,</em> and <em>ALDH2</em> are with the same SNP that has been reported to have an association with colorectal cancer. These genome-wide associations
                     are listed at <a href="http://www.genome.gov/gwastudies/">http://www.genome.gov/gwastudies/</a>, but because the causal genetic variant is not known in most reports, there may be some instances of wrong assignment of
                     genes to these observed associations. Nevertheless, reports of robust associations with other diseases in 11 genes (35% of
                     genes showing association with colorectal cancer and 17% of all meta-analyzed genes) highlight the importance of pleiotropy.
                     This presumably reflects a common underlying pathological process underlying these conditions, for example, between (i) colorectal,
                     breast, esophageal, bladder, and prostate cancers (<em>CND1</em>, <em>MYC</em>, <em>GSTM1</em>) and (ii) colorectal cancer and inflammatory bowel disease (<em>CDH1</em>). Other pleiotropic links are less readily explained but may yield clues to novel underlying pathological processes.
                  </p>
                  
                  <p id="p-93" class="indent">We have conducted a comprehensive exercise to capture and meta-analyze all SNP data for variants with MAFs in the range 0.01–0.49.
                     The analysis clearly identifies 16 variants for which there is robust evidence of impact on risk of colorectal cancer; 23
                     variants for which further evidence through international collaboration should be generated; and 20 variants for which the
                     overall evidence does not support association and on which further research is not warranted. With increasing availability
                     of data from multiple SNPs, it is clear that studies to test associations must achieve very high levels of statistical stringency.
                     It has been suggested that even for candidate SNPs, that statistical support should reach genome-wide thresholds. Nonetheless,
                     the analysis here provides a resource for mining available data and puts into context the sample sizes required for the identification
                     of true associations. This study highlights a number of SNP associations that could be incorporated into genetic risk–prediction
                     algorithms as further risk factors are identified and highlights the loci at which further research effort should be targeted.
                     The data are all lodged on the CRCgene database (<a href="http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/">http://www.chs.med.ed.ac.uk/CRCgene/</a>).
                  </p>
                  
               </div>

                  <h2>References 
                     
                  </h2>
